:transcript: н╨о┘н╫А╫╦о┌ н⌡н╠н╨н╠н╞н╫н╥о┌.
:start_offset: 0
:end_offset: 15

According to Aristotle, the Laconian dogs were crossbred with foxes (н╣л⌠н╬ н╠л⌠н╩о┴л│о─н╣н╨н©о┌ н╨н╠н╧л─ н╨о┘н╫н©л─о┌ н©н╧л■ н⌡н╠н╨о┴н╫н╧н╨н©н╧л│, Hist. Anim. 8. 28, p. 607 a ). He describes them as a small breed, with long nostrils and keen scent (н©л■л│о┐о┴н╫ н©н╧л■ н╪о┘н╨о└н╥м┌о│н╣о┌ н╪н╠н╨о│н©н╧л│, н©н╧л⌠м┌н©н╫ о└о┴м┌н╫ н⌡н╠н╨о┴н╫н╧н╨о┴м┌н╫ н╨о┘н╫н╧н╢н╧л│о┴н╫, н©л⌠о┐о├о│н╠н╫о└н╧н╨н╠л│: Gener. Anim. 5. 2, . 781 b 9). They were the best huntin ogs, as Pindar testifies, fr. 106 : н╠л⌠о─ н╓н╠о┘л─нЁн╣л│о└н©н╧н© н╪н╣л─н╫ н⌡н╠л│н╨н╠н╧н╫н╠н╫ н╧н╣л⌠о─н╧л─ н╦н╥о│о┐н╧л─ н╨о┘л│н╫н╠ о└о│н╣л│о├н╣н╧н╫ [v. l. о└о│н╣л│о┤н╣н╧н╫] о─о┘н╨н╧н╫о┴л│о└н╠о└н©н╫ н╣л■о│о─н╣о└н©л│н╫. The Molossian dog is often associated with the Laconian (Hor. Epod. 6. 5 Molossus aut fulvus Laco: Verg. G. 3. 405 Velocis Spartae catulos acremqu Molossum)); but Aristotle tells us that the Molossian breed had no special merit for sporting purposes ; its best product was the large sheep-dog. The chief points common to the Laconian and Molossian breeds were courage and pertinacity (н╣н╧л⌠н╫н╢о│н╧л│н╠ and о├н╧н╩н©о─н©н╫н╧л│н╠, Arist. Hist. An. 9. 1, . 608 a 31). The use of the feminine gender by Sophocles here may be illustrated by Aristotle's remark,Б─■н╠н╧ н⌡н╠л│н╨н╠н╧н╫н╠н╧ н╨о┘л│н╫н╣о┌ н╠н╧л■ н╦н╥л│н╩н╣н╧н╠н╧ н╣о┘л⌠о├о┘н╣л│о┐о└н╣о│н╠н╧ о└о┴м┌н╫ н╠л⌠о│о│н╣л│н╫о┴н╫ н╣н╧л⌠о┐н╧л│н╫,i.e., Б─≤of a finer intelligence Б─≥ (ib. p. 608 a 27)..Б─■Cp. Shakesp. Midsummer-Niл│ht's Dream 4. 1. 124, where Theseus says, My hounds are bned out of the Spartan kind | ..A cry moe tuneable | Was never hola'a' to, nor cheer'd with honn, | In Crete, in Sparta, nor in Thhessaly.

The eneral sense of н╠л⌠л│о─н©о─о└н©о┌ here is, Б─≤far rom my sight; the question is whether this means, () Б─≤seen only at a distance,Б─≥ Б─≤dimly seenБ─≥; or (2) Б─≤unseen.Б─≥ I formerly preferred the first view, for which we may compare [Ph. 467,](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0011.tlg006:467,) о─н╩н©о┘м┌н╫ н╪н╥л─ н⌠н╬ н╠л⌠о─н©л│о─о└н©о┘ н╪н╠м┌н╩н╩н©н╫ н╥л⌠л─ Б─≥нЁнЁо┘л│н╦н╣н╫ о┐н╨н©о─н╣н╧м┌н╫, 'to watch the weather near our ship, rather than from afr.Б─≥ But I now feel two difficulties which it involves. (1) The emphasis on voice and thoughtБ─■о├н╦н╣л│нЁн╪н╠, о├о┴л│н╫н╥н╪н╠, н╬о┘н╫н╠о│о─н╠л│н╤о┴ о├о│н╣н╫н╧л│Б─■is so strong as to imply that he doses not see her, even at a distance. (2) There ca ce no doubt that she was visible to the audience. She was probably on the н╦н╣н©н╩н©нЁн╣н╧м┌н©н╫,Б─■u sort of platform, which projected from the wings, at the back of the proscenium, and close to its upper edge. If, then, [Odysseus](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q47231) spoke of her as 'seen only afar,Б─≥Б─■a dim vision in the clouds,Б─■the effect would be scarcely happy for the spectators, whose eyees could measure the actual distance between goddess and hero. On the other hand, there would seem nothing strange in her remaining invisible to him. In the [Hippolytus,](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q375786) [Aphroditeл─](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q35500) speaks the prologue, and was certainly visible to the spectators; but not to [Hippolytus,](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q338201) who says, н╨н╩о┘л│о┴н╫ н╪н╣л─н╫ н╠о┘л⌠н╢н╥л│н╫, н©л⌠л│н╪н╪н╠ н╢Б─≥ н©о┘л⌠о┤ н©л■о│о┴м┌н╫ о└н©л─ о┐н©л│н╫. Whe [Ajax](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q172725) comes forth, he, indeed, appears to see her (v. 91, о┴л■м┌ о┤н╠н╧м┌о│Б─≥ н▒л⌠н╦н╠л│н╫н╠, н╨.о└. н╩, ; while [to Tecmessa](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q10796858) she is invisible (301). But this, again, would not be inconsistent with Greek belief. In [Il. 22. 277](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0012.tlg001:22.277) [Athena](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q37122) restores the spear to [Achilles,](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q41746) yet is unseen by [Hector.](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q159666) In [Il. 1. 198](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0012.tlg001:1.198) she appears to [Achilles,](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q41746) but the others see her not. For н╠л⌠л│о─н©о─о└н©о┌ as = Б─≤out of sight,Б─≥ cp. [El. 1488](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0011.tlg005:1488) (of [Aegisthus)](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q212091) о─о│н©л│н╦н╣о┌ [ н╠л⌠л│о─н©о─о└н©н╫ н╥л■н╪о┴м┌н╫ : Dionys. Hal. 2. 54 н╣л⌠н╫ н╠л⌠о─н©л│о─о└о┴м┘ о└н╧л│н╦н╣н╫о└н╠н╧ о└н©л─н╫ о┤н╠л│о│н╠н╨н╠н╠ ('in a place out of sight). It may be added that we might suppose [Athena](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q37122) to be invisible to [Odysseus](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q47231) nov, without necessarily excluding the idea that she becomes visible to him at a later moment in the dialogue. Thus in [Eur. Hipp. 1391](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0006.tlg005:1391) the presence of [Artemis](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q39503) is known to [Hippolytus](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q338201) only by a divine fragrance, though to the spectators she is doubtless visible; but a little later he beholds her (14).)Б─■See Appendix.

The trumet meant here was in form like the oman, Б─■straight, gradually increasin in diameter, and ending in a bell-shap aperture (н╨о┴л│н╢о┴н╫). Б─≤Tyrrhenian,Б─≥ a freuent epithet of the trumpet [(Aesch. um. 567,](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0085.tlg007:567,) [Eur. Phoem. 1377,](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0006.tlg015:1377,) [Verg. Aen. 8. 526, etc.),](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:latinLit:phi0690.phi003:526,etc.),) perhaps means no more than that the instrument was first brought to [Europe](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q46) by Tyrrhenian pirates,Б─■the н╩н╥м┘о┐о└н©о┐н╠н╩о─н╧нЁн╨о└н╠н╧л│ н©f Menander (frag. incert. 399). The Tyrrheni, according to a tradition for which [Herodotus](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q26825) is the earliiest witness (1. 94), were of Lydian origin; and the bronze trumpet may have been a Lydian invention (see Dict. Ant. Б─≤Tuba). [Homer](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q6691) mentions the trumpet only in similes, as when the voice of [Achilles](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q41746) is likened to it [(ll. 18. 219, etc.).](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0012.tlg001:18.219,etc.).) But the Greeks must have had it as early at least as the time of the Dorian conquest, to judge from the legends heard by [Pausanias](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q192931) at [Argos](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q13533353) (2. 21, б╖ 3) where there was a shrine of н▒л⌠н╦н╥н╫н╠м┌ нён╠л│н╩о─н╧нЁн╬.Б─■See Avvendix. As to the gender of н╨о┴л│н╢о┴н╫, [Arist. De Sens. 6 p. 446 22](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0086.tlg041:6p.44622) has о└н╥м┌о┌ н╨о┴л│н╢о┴н╫н©о┌ (bell). In [Ar. Pax 1078](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0019.tlg005:1078) the words о┤о┴ н╨о┴л│н╢о┴н╫ н╠л⌠н╨н╠н╩н╠н╫н╦н╧л│о┌ are said to mean a kind of hound. But н╨о┴л│н╢о┴н╫ (bell) is masc. with [Thuc.,](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q41683) [Strabo,](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q45936) [Plutarch,](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q41523) [Diodorus](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q171241) and Lucian.

But the poet wished to render the preparation for the heroБ─≥s entrance as impressive as possible; and chose, therefore, to represent [OdysseusБ─■a](http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q47231) brave manБ─■as still uneasy, until the assurance given to him had been reeated in a yet more explicit and emphatic form.

T. 1436.](https://scaife.perseus.org/reader/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0006.tlg013:1436.) The Homeric vocative of н╧л⌠л│н╠о┌ is always н▒н╧л⌠м┌н╠н╫ (н╠л├), Б─■formed on the analogy of te voc. from stems in н©н╫о└, as н о│н╣л│н©н╫ (stem н о│н╣н©н╫о└). The vocatives н═н©н©о┘н╩о┘н╢н╠л│н╪н╠ (Il. 12. 231), н⌡н╠н©н╢н╠л│н╪н╠ (Od. 8. 1441) are exceptions, due perhaps to a sense that here the ending -н╠н╫ might suggest an accusative.

(which has needlessly been suspected, cr. n.) is interpreted by vv. 127 f., and more immediately by the contrast with о└н©о┘л─о┌ о┐о┴л│о├о│н©н╫н╠о┌ : it means those who, like Ajax, are led into н╢о┘о┐о┐н╣л│н╡н╣н╧н╠ by overweening self-reliance or pride.

@urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0011.tlg003.ajmc-lj:134-200
:citable_urn: urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0011.tlg003.ajmc-jeb:134-200
:image_paths: ["sophoclesplaysa05campgoog/sophoclesplaysa05campgoog_0115/full/max/0/default.png"]
:overlays: [{"width":325,"page_id":"sophoclesplaysa05campgoog_0115","px":291,"py":3308,"height":44},{"width":1167,"page_id":"sophoclesplaysa05campgoog_0115","px":221,"py":3302,"height":1171},{"width":1158,"page_id":"sophoclesplaysa05campgoog_0115","px":1477,"py":2326,"height":846}]
:page_ids: ["sophoclesplaysa05campgoog_0115"]

Parodos, in two main parts, vi., (1) 134Б─■171, the anapaestic marching-song, or parodos proper, sung by the Chorus asthey enter ; (2) 172200, the lyric ode, sung after they have taken up their position in the orchestra. This is the form of Parodos used by Aeschylus in the Persae, the Supplices, and the Agamemnon.. Nearest to this early and simple form is that in which lyric strophes are intermingled with anapaestic systems. A parodos of the latter type is found in the Prometheus Vinctus, the Antigone, and the Philoctetes. The anapaestic march consists here of 38 verses; in the Aeschylean Supplices, of 40. It is much longer in the Fersae (64 v.), and in the Agamemnon (63). The Chorus of fifteen Salaminian sailors enter the orchestra from the spectatorБ─≥s right (as they come from their quarters in the Greek camp). They march in three files (о┐о└н©н╧м┌о┤н©н╧) of five men each. At the close of the anapaests (v. 171), they form themselves in three ranks (н╤о┘нЁн╠л│) of five each, facing the proscenium. It can scarcely be doubted that the anapaests were chanted by the whole Chorus, and not by the coryphaeus alone (see A. Malller, Gr. Biл┬hnenaterthuл┬mer, p. 217, n. 2).

---

Il. 13. 67 н╓н╣н╩н╠н╪о┴л│н╫н╧н©н╫ о┘н╧л⌠н©л│н╫. The followers of Ajax call upon him to come forth from his tent, Б─■where, for some days previously, he had remained in sullen secluson (194f), Б─■and to refute this dire rumour of his onslaught on the cattle. the plain Б─≤wild with horses,Б─≥ i.e., on which great numbers of horses (out at pasture) disport themselves. this form is quoted by Hesychius from the poet's lost Hipponos (Soph. fr. 282). In O. T. 171 н╠л⌠н╩н╣л│н╬н╣о└н╠н╧ is a fut.: which, like this aor., seems to come from a stem н╠л⌠н╩н╣н╨ rather than from н╠л⌠н╩н╣н╬ (whence the pres. н╠л⌠н╩н╣л│н╬о┴): see n. there.

Homeric н╥л■м┌ о│л■н╠ is sometimes interrogative (Il. 7. 446), but occurs also where there is no question (12, 164 н╥л■л│ о│л■ н╫н╧. н©н╥ о│л■н╠ is interogative in Pind. P. 9. 40, I. 7. 3: but not in P. 11. 38. This н╥л⌠м┌ is not the contracted form of the dislunctive н╥л⌠н╣л│ (Il. 6. 378 ff. н╥л⌠н╣л│ ...н╥л⌠м┌), which was not used in direct question. The sailors ask whether Artemis or Ares can have driven Ajax to such a deed. In connection with their chief, the deities of hunting and war naturally occur to them. They do not think of Athena.

= н╓н╠о┘о│н©о─н©л│н╩н©о┌: for the form, cp. Pind. O. 3. 26 н⌡н╠о└н©о┘м┌о┌ н╧л■о─о─н©о┐н©л│н╠ н╦о┘нЁн╠л│о└н╥о│: Eur. Ion 1478 н⌠н©о│нЁн©о├н©л│н╫н╠. There were two cults of Artemis, originally quite distinct. (1) The н╓н╠о┘о│н©о─н©л│н╩н©о┌ was a goddess who rules over bulls,Б─■one of the numerous attributes of Artemis in her relation to wild animals: cp. н╠н╧л⌠о─н©л│н╩н©о┌, н╧о─о─н©о─н©л│н╩н©о┌, н©н╧л⌠н©о─н©л│н╩н©о┌. A Macedonian silver coin (from Amphipolis) shows her riding on a prancing bull, and carrying a torc in either hand. On another coin she appears (with the inscr. н╓н▒н╔н║н÷н═н÷н÷нё) carrying two torches, and with the horns off a bull growing out of her shoulders : these horns were meant to suggest the crescent moon. The cult of the Taurolos was purely Greek, and had nothing erce or cruel about it. (2) The cult of Artemis н╓н╠о┘о│н╧н╨н╥л│ (or н╓н╠о┘о│о┴л│) had orgiastic elements; it was of Asiatic origin, and belonged to a primitive stage of natureworship. The ritual was a bloody one, and in early times involved human sacrifice. This was the Artemis whose cult was said to have been brought to Brauron in Attica, from the Tauric Chersonese, by Iphigeneia and Orestes. It is possible that Sophocles here was thinking only of the н╓н╠о┘о│н©о─н©л│н╩н©о┌ proper, and names her simply because bulls had been among the victims of Ajax. On the other hand, the savage nature of the bloody onslaught might suggest that she was associated in his thought with the н╓н╠о┘о│н╧н╨н╥л│. Such an association occurs as early as Eur. I. T. 1457 where the Brauronian Artemis (н╣л⌠о─о┴л│н╫о┘н╪н©н╫ нЁн╥м┌м┘ н╓н╠о┘о│н╧н╨н╥м┌о┌, 12454) is called н╓н╠о┘о│н©о─н©л│н╩н©о┌. The dithyrambic poet Timotheus of Miletus (c. 400 B.c.) addressed the Ephesian Artemis as н╪н╠н╧н╫н╠л│н╢н╠, н╦о┘н╧н╠л│н╢н╠, о├н©н╧н╡н╠л│н╢н╠, н╩о┘о┐о┐н╠л│н╢н╠ (Bergk fr. 1). See Appendix.

---

The parallels adduced are such as н╥л■ н╣л■о┴м┌м┘н╠, н╥л■ н╣л■о┴н╦н╧н╫н╥л│, н╥л■л─ н©л⌠о┬н╧л│н╠, н╥л■ о─о│о┴н╧л⌠м┌н╠, о└н╥л─н╫ н╦н╣о│н╣н╧л│н╥н╫ (in summer,Б─≥ Her. l. 189), о└н╥л─н╫ о┤н╣н╧н╪н╣о│н╧н╫н╥л│н╫ (id. 1. 202). There was indeed a tendency in Greek idiom to make substantive out of fem. adjectives; i.e. to use the fem. adj. alone, whenever the subst. (such as нЁн╥м┌, н©л■н╢н©л│о┌, о┴л■л│о│н╠, н╪н©н╧м┌о│н╠, н╢н╧л│н╨н╥) could readily be underБ─■ stood. And that tendency must be considered here. No tolerable emendation has been made. н╥л⌠о│н╣н╪н╧л│н╠о┌, Б─≤stillness,Б─≥ though possible, is very feeble. н╣о┘л⌠н╪н╠о│н╧л│н╠о┌ (2н╥о┘л■н╪н╠о│н╣н╧л│н╠н╣о┌), which Nauck adopt, is directly contrary to the sense; for, even before the dread rumour arose, the Chorus had been disquieted by the long seclusion and inaction of Ajax (194 f). н╠л⌠н╪н╪н©о│н╧л│н╠о┌, without some further definition (such as о└н╥м┌о┌ о─о│н╧м┌н╫), would be too vague. And the words н╫о┘м┌н╬ н╥л⌠л│н╢н╣ suggest that there was some mention of day. On the whole, the traditional reading, though difficult, is less open to objection than any remedy which has been proposed. Finaly, о└н╠л─ н╢н╣ (. н╡н©о└н╠л─ reters to oм┌н╫.Б─■The antithesis to о└н╥л─н╫ н╪н╣л─н╫ is given by о└н╠л─ н╢н╣л│, not (as some have thought) by н╢о┘л│н© н╢.-н╣л⌠л│о┐о┴, Б─°within the house,Б─≥ refers to all the incidents that followed his arrival (н╥л⌠л│н╩о┘н╦н╣)). Schneidewin wrongly joins н╣л⌠л│о┐о┴ н╤н╣ (Astach totБ─≥), comparing Aesch. Ag. 1343 о─н╣л│о─н╩н╥нЁн╪н╠н╧ н╨н╠н╧о│н╧л│н╠н╫ о─н╩н╥нЁн╥л─н╫ н╣л⌠л│о┐о┴. But о┐о├н╠л│н╤н╣ denotes Б─≤cuting the throat, not 'stabbingБ─≥; cp. 298.Б─■н╣л⌠о─н╧л─ нЁн╠н╧л│н╠о┌, where they stood upon the floor; as distinguished from those animals which he caught up and rent asunder. о─н╩н╣о┘о│н©н╨н©о─о┴м┌н╫, slashing at their sides with his sword: a compound like н╦н╠н╩н╠о└о└н©н╨н©о─н╣н╧м┌н╫ (Ar. Ev.. 830), н╪н╣о└н╣о┴о│н©н╨н©о─н╣н╧м┌н╫ (Pax 92). 237 (. н╠л⌠о│нЁн╧л│о─н©н╢н╠о┌: for the rt. ARG, denoting Б─≤brightness,Б─≥ and its derivatives, cp. O. C. 670 n. As Ajax purposed to og Odysseus (110), the ram which suffers that fate here might naturally be identified with him. The first ram, which is beheaded at once, might then represent Agamemnon; and Menelauis would here be ignored. On this view, however, there is a discrepancy with verses 97Б─■1 30, since both the Atreidae are there supposed to be dead, while Odysseus has still to suffer. Here one ram (Agamemnon) is slain, and the other (Odysseus) flogged, in rapid succession. If the two rams are the two Atreidae, then Odysseus is ignored here. This would be consistent both with v. 97Б─■ 110 and with 298Б─■306. The flogging of the second ram here would correspond with the words о└н©о┘л─о┌ н╢н╣л─ н╢н╣о┐н╪н╧л│н©о┘о┌ [ н╥л⌠м┘н╨н╧л│н╤н╣н╦' in 299 f. Then came the colloquy of Ajax with Athena (301); after which, he slowly regained his sanity,Б─■before he had inflicted the lash upon Odysseus. That the Б─≤two ramsБ─≥ are here the royal lrethren might be suggested by the analogy of the two eagles in Ag. 11. On the other hand, the special ferocity with which Ajax treats the second ram rather indicates that it represents Odysseus. There would be no point in so distinguishing Menelauis from Agamemnon. On the whole, it seems most probable that the oet meant Agamemnon and Odysseus, ut was careless of strict consistency with vV97Б─■110. 288 н╨н╠о├н╠н╩н╥л─н╫ н╨н╠н╧л─ нЁн╩о┴м┌о┐о┐н╠н╫ н╠л⌠л│н╨о│н╠н╫. He first shears off the head; then, still holding it, while the carcass drops to the ground, cuts off the end of the tongue; and next hurls both head and tongue from him. н╠л⌠л│н╨о│н╠ нЁн╩о┴м┌о┐о┐н╠ can mean the extremity, the tip, of the tongue, as in Theocr. 9. 30 н╣л⌠о─н╧л─ нЁн╩о┴л│о┐о┐н╠о┌ н╠л⌠л│н╨о│н╠о┌: so in Ph. 748 н╠л⌠л│н╨о│н©н╫ о─н©л│н╢н╠ is the heel. It could not mean, Б─≤the toongue from the roots,Б─≥ нЁн╩о┴м┌о┐о┐н╠н╫ о─о│о┘н╪н╫н╥л│н╫ (Il. 5. 292).Б─■It was customary to cutt out the tongues of animals slain for sacrifice, and to offer them separately (Od. 3. 32: Ar. Pax 1060). But I doubt whether there is any reference here to the sacrificial custom ; the act of Ajax rather expresses merely fierce hatred of a slanderer.

The implied antithesis is with what he might feel if told of things which he had done elsewhere. but which he coud not see. Cp. 1131, 1156. 330 н╧о┴л─ о─н╠н╧м┌ о─н╠н╧м┌.. One of the sigs that he is resolved to die is the way in which his thoughts dwell upon his son; he cannot rest till he sees him (530Б─■544). The child is the heir of his fame, and may live to clear it of the stain (556 f.). As Eurysaces is not at once brought to him, he next cries (342), н╓н╣о┘м┌н╨о│н©н╫ н╨н╠н╩о┴м┌: Teucer is his second thought, for to Teucer he will commit the care of his son (562). Tecmessa, at least, did not so take the words. 340 1. о┴л⌠л│н╪н©н╧ о└н╠л│н╩н╠н╧н╫н╫ : the nom. as in 800, El. 788, and oft.Б─■н∙о┘о│о┘л│о┐н╠н╨н╣о┌, so named from the shield of Ajax (19 ): cp. 574 n. Here I will briefly state what seem the main points of the vroblem. (1) The antistrophic verses, 423Б─■427 (н╣л⌠н╬н╣о│о┴м┌.... о─о│н©л│н╨н╣н╧н╪н╠н╧), appear sound. (3) If, then, we assume that the antistrophe is sound, we may conclude that the fault in the strophe lies within the words н╣н╧л⌠ о└н╠л─ н╪н╣л─н╫ о├н╦н╧л│н╫н╣н╧, о├н╧л│н╩н©н╧, о└н©н╧м┌о┐н╢Б─≥ н©л■н╪н©о┘м┌ о─н╣л│н╩н╠н╫. The proper sense of н╠л⌠н╫н╠о─н╫н©н╥л│ is (1) Б─≤recovery of breathБ─≥; or (2) = н╣о┘л⌠о┐о─н╫н©н╥л│ as opp. to н╣л⌠н╨о─о│н©о┴л│, the drawing in -of breath. ere, however, it means merely о─н╫н©н╥л│, as =Б─≥the breath of life.Б─≥ Cp. Eur. Or. 421 н╪н╥о└о│н©л─о┌ н©н╧л⌠л│о┤н©н╫о└н╠н╧ о─н╫н©н╠н╧л│. 417 f. нён╨н╠н╪н╠л│н╫н╢о│н╧н©н╧.о│л■н©н╠н╧л│: cp. Il. 2. 467 н╣л⌠н╫ н╩н╣н╧н╪о┴м┌н╫н╧ нён╨н╠н╪н╠н╫н╢о│н╧л│о┴м┘ н╠л⌠н╫-н╦н╣н╪н©л│н╣н╫о└н╧. The Scamander, still called the Mendere, is the most important river of the Troad. Rising in Ida, it flows through the central plain which now bears the name of the town Bairamitch, and thence passes northward into the plain of Troy. On the confines between the two plains, where the river winds in large curves through deep gorges in limestone rocks, its scenery is striking. From the south border of th Trojan plain to the Hellespont, the Mendere has a course of between seven and eight miles.

Ant. 463 н©л■л│о┐о└н╧о┌ нЁн╠л─о│ н╣л⌠н╫ о─н©н╩н╩н©н╧м┌о┐н╧н╫, о┴л■о┌ н╣л⌠нЁо┴л│, н╨н╠н╨н©н╧м┌о┌ н╤н╥м┌м┘, о─о┴м┌о┌ н©л■л│н╢' н©о┘л⌠о┤н╧л─ н╨н╠о└н╦н╠н╫о┴л─н╫ н╨н╣л│о│н╢н©о┌ о├н╣л│о│н╣н╧ ; fr. 866 н©л■л│о┐о└н╧о┌ нЁн╠л─о│ н╣л⌠н╫ н╨н╠н╨н©н╧м┌о┐н╧н╫ н╧л⌠н╪н╣н╧л│о│н╣н╧ н╡н╧л│н©о┘,| н╥л⌠л─ н╢н╣н╧н╩н©л│о┌ н╣л⌠о┐о└н╧н╫ н╥л⌠л─ н╢о┘о┐н╠л│н╩нЁн╥о└н©о┌ о├о│н╣л│н╫н╠о┌. 475 f. о─н╠о│' н╥л■м┌н╪н╠о│ н╥л■н╪н╣л│о│н╠, 2the successiveБ─≥ (not, Б─≤alternateБ─≥) Б─≤daysБ─≥: the series formed by placing each new day at the side of its predecessors, as н╠л⌠н╫н╥л─о│ о─н╠о│' н╠л⌠л│н╫н╢о│н╠ could be said of forming men in rank. The sense is: *When a man's miseries are unbroken, о└н╧л│ о└н╣л│о│о─н╣н╧н╫ н╣л⌠л│о┤н╣н╧ н╥л■н╪н╣л│о│н╠ о─н╠о│' н╥л⌠м┌н╪н╠о│, what power to please him has each successive day, о─о│н©о┐н╦н╣н╧л│о┐н╠ ((н╠о┘л⌠о└н©л─н╫ о└о┴м┌м┘ н╨н╠о└н╦н╠н╫н╣н╧м┌н╫, when it has brought him close up to death, н╨н╠н╫н╠н╦н╣н╧м┌о┐н╠ о└н©о┘м┌ н╨н╠о└н╦н╠н╫н╣н╧м┌н╫, and then again moved him back from death? Death is the boundary-line (нЁо│н╠н╪н╪н╥л│) on the field of life: Eur. El. 955 о─о│н╧л─н╫ н╠л⌠л─н╫ о─н╣л│н╩н╠о┌ | нЁо│н╠н╪н╪н╥м┌о┌ н╧л■л│н╨н╥о└н╠н╧ н╨н╠н╧л─ о└н╣л│н╩н©о┌ н╨н╠л│н╪о┬н╥м┘ н╡н╧л│н©о┘ (mors altima linea rerum). The man who is captive to evil fortune is like a о─н╣о┐о┐н©л│о┌ on the draughtboard, at one moment moved close up to the line, and then again withdrawn from it a little; but it is death, after all,Б─■death and nothing else,Б─■that awaits him (о└н©о┘м┌ нЁн╣ н╨н╠о└н╦н╠н╫н╣н╧м┌н╫). This is not the platitude, Б─≤all men must dieБ─≥, but a thought suggested by the case of the man Б─≤whose woes are unvarying.Б─≥ Life has nothing in store for him; the question is mere whether he is to die at once, or to have a short and wretched reprieve. This is brought out by v. 479: н╨н╠н╩о┴м┌о┌ н╤н╥м┌н╫ is denied to him; and that being so, the next best thing is to die honourably and quickly-н╨н╠н╩о┴м┌о┌ о└н╣н╦н╫н╥н╨н╣л│н╫н╠н╧.

Destiny, working through Ajax, ravaged her coucty. Destiny in some other form brought her father and mother to the grave. The poet did not wish to represent Tecmessa as living with the man who had slain her parents; and so followed, or invented, a legend which ascribed their deaths to a different agency. Throughout this speech, the Homeric scene between Hector and Andromacheл─ is present to the mind of Sophocles. Б─≤and verily Б─≥) introduces a new fact or thought ; as in Tragedy it often announces a new comer (I168. The practice of burying a warriorБ─≥s arms with him was a primitive Aryan custom ; witness the swords found in the graves at Mycenae. Sophocles in this play е©ollows the legend that the body of Ajax was interred, and not, as was the ordinary practice in the heroic age, burned (cp. 1166 n.). Tennyson's poem, The LotosEaters, begins thus Б─■Б─≤Courage IБ─² he said, and pointed toward the land, | Б─°This mounting wave will roll us shoreward soon.Б─² | In the afternoon they came unto a land 2 In which it seemed always afternoon.Б─≥ The repetition of the wordБ─╒ Б─≥landБ─≥ there was deliberate, being designed to suggest languor. Sophocles doubtless had no such conscious aim here; he was simply indifferent, as so often, to the recurrence (0. C. 554 n.); but something of a like effect is actually produced. Cp. I. 5. 451 н╢н╧м┌н©н╧ н▒л⌠о┤н╠н╧н©н╧л│. 046-002 Second н╣л⌠о─н╣н╧о┐н©л│н╢н╧н©н╫. Ajax comes forth from the tent, and addresses his faithful friends. The spech translates his unshaken purpose into the terms of his altered mood; yet so as to veil his meaning from the hearers, and to make them think that not only the mood, but the purpose, is changed (See Introduction, б╖ 12.) Tecmessa who left the scene at 595, now returns, entering on the right of the spectators, and leading her child. Their presence adds effect to v. 653 ; and Ajax addresses her at v. 684. 646I. н©л■ н╪н╠н╨о│н©л─о┌.. о┤о│н©л│н╫н©о┌: see Her. 5. 9, quoted in n. on 86.Б─■ о├о┘л│н╣н╧ о└Б─≥ н╨.о└.н╩.: the emphasis is on the power of time to enfeeble and destroy, rather than to produce; but the first clause serves to bri the second into relief, and is co-ordinat with it, as in Ant. 1112 н╠н╠о┘л⌠о└н©л│о┌ о└Б─≥ н╣л⌠л│н╢н╥о┐н╠ н╨н╠н╧л─ о─н╠о│о┴л─н╫ н╣л⌠н╨н╩о┘л│о┐н©н╪н╠н╧.The rash change of to о├н╠н╧л│н╫н╣н╧ overlooks the poet's tenency to vary his phrase in such cases ; e.g. O. Od. 20. 202 о┘л⌠н╨ н╣л⌠н╩н╣н╠н╧л│о│н╣н╧о┌ н╠л⌠л│н╫н╢о│н╠о┌.. | н╪н╧о┐нЁн╣л│н╪н╣н╫н╠н╧ н╨н╠н╨н©л│о└н╥о└н╧. Cp. I. 5. 451 н╢н╧м┌н©н╧ н▒л⌠о┤н╧н©н╧л│. 046-602 Second н╣л⌠о─н╣н╧о┐н©л│н╢н╧н©н╫. Ajax comes forth from the tent, and addresses his faithful friends. The speech translates his unshaken purpose into the terms of his altered mood; yet so as to veil his meaning from the hearers, and to make them think that not only the mood, but the purpose, is changed. (See Introduction, б╖ 12.) Tecmessa who left the scene at 595, now returns, entering on the right of the spectators, and leading her child. Their presence adds effect to v. 653 ; and Ajax addresses her at v. 684. 646 f. н©л■ н╪н╠н╨о│н©л─о┌ . о┤о│н©л│н╫н©о┌: see Her. 5. 9, quoted in n. on 86.lБ─■ о├о┘л│н╣н╧ о└Б─≥ н╨.о└.н╩.: the emphasis is on the power of time to enfeeble and destroy, rather than to produce; but the first clause serves to brin the second into relief, and is co-ordinate with it, as in Ant. 1112 н╠о┘л⌠о└н©л│о┌ о└Б─≥ н╣л⌠л│н╢н╥о┐н╠ н╨н╠н╧л─ о─н╠о│о┴л─н╫ н╣л⌠н╨н╩о┘л│о┐н©н╪н╠н╧.The rash change of н╣н╧ to о├н╠н╧л│н╫н╣н╧ overlooks the poet's tenency to vary his phrase in such cases ; e.g. O. :overlays: [{"width":478,"page_id":"sophoclesplaysa05campgoog_0192","px":1880,"py":2978,"height":53},{"width":1162,"page_id":"sophoclesplaysa05campgoog_0192","px":1639,"py":2981,"height":976}]

:transcript: А╪─н╩н╩й╪ н╠А╫░о└А╫╦ н╨.о└.н╩.
:start_offset: 0
:end_offset: 38

So ClytaemnestraБ─≥s gifts to Agamemnon's tomb are to be laid up for her with the gods below : н╨н╣н╧н╪н╥л│н╩н╧' н╠о┘л⌠о└н╥м┌м┘ о└н╠о┘м┌о└н╠ о┐о┴м┘н╤н╣л│о┐н╦о┴ н╨н╠л│о└о┴ (El. 438). An ingenious critic, J. Geel, writing on vv 6466Б─■692 in Mnemosyne (II. pp. 200-208, 1853), wishes to omit this verse, because it implies the only direct falsehood in the speech; Ajax was not going to bury his sword under ground. But why should not Ajax think of the sword as destined to be buried in his grave ? The shield alone was excepted from the direction which he gave in 577 : о└н╠л─ н╢' н╠л⌠л│н╩н╩н╠ о└н╣о┘л│о┤н╥ н╨н©н╧л│Б─≥ н╣л⌠н╪н©н╧л─ о└н╣н╦н╠л│о┬н╣о└н╠н╧.
